## Chapter 2: Transmission Lines:

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we discussed the basics of superconductivity and its applications. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the topic of transmission lines, which are an essential component in many superconducting systems. Transmission lines are used to transfer electrical signals from one point to another with minimal loss of energy. They are widely used in various applications, including communication systems, power transmission, and medical imaging.

In this chapter, we will cover the fundamentals of transmission lines, including their basic structure, behavior, and characteristics. We will also discuss the different types of transmission lines, such as coaxial, microstrip, and waveguide, and their applications in superconducting systems. Additionally, we will explore the concept of impedance matching and its importance in transmission line design.

Furthermore, we will also touch upon the topic of superconducting transmission lines, which have gained significant attention in recent years due to their potential for high-speed data transmission and low power loss. We will discuss the advantages and challenges of using superconducting materials in transmission lines and their potential applications.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to transmission lines, covering their basic principles, types, and applications. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a better understanding of transmission lines and their role in superconducting systems. 




### Section: 2.1 Perfect Diamagnetism:

In the previous chapter, we discussed the basics of superconductivity and its applications. In this section, we will explore the concept of perfect diamagnetism, which is a fundamental property of superconductors.

#### 2.1a London's equation for perfect diamagnetism

London's equation is a fundamental equation in the study of superconductivity. It describes the behavior of superconductors in the presence of an external magnetic field. The equation is named after the physicist Fritz London, who first proposed it in 1935.

London's equation is given by:

$$
\frac{\partial \mathbf{B}}{\partial t} = -\nabla \times \mathbf{E}
$$

where $\mathbf{B}$ is the magnetic field, $\mathbf{E}$ is the electric field, and $t$ is time. This equation describes the relationship between the magnetic field and the electric field in a superconductor. It states that the change in magnetic field over time is equal to the curl of the electric field.

In the case of perfect diamagnetism, the magnetic field is completely expelled from the superconductor, and the electric field is zero. This means that the left-hand side of London's equation is also zero. Therefore, we can rewrite London's equation for perfect diamagnetism as:

$$
\nabla \times \mathbf{E} = 0
$$

This equation is known as the London equation for perfect diamagnetism. It describes the behavior of superconductors in the absence of any external magnetic field. In this case, the magnetic field is completely expelled from the superconductor, and the electric field is zero.

The London equation for perfect diamagnetism is a crucial concept in the study of superconductivity. It helps us understand the behavior of superconductors in the presence of an external magnetic field and provides a basis for further exploration of superconducting systems. In the next section, we will discuss the different types of transmission lines and their applications in superconducting systems.








### Section: 2.1 Perfect Diamagnetism:

Superconductivity is a phenomenon that has been studied extensively since its discovery in 1911. It is a state of matter where certain materials exhibit zero electrical resistance and perfect diamagnetism when cooled below a critical temperature. This critical temperature, known as the transition temperature, is different for each superconductor and is a key factor in determining its suitability for various applications.

#### 2.1a London's equations

London's equations, named after the physicists Fritz London and Walter Meissner, are fundamental to understanding the behavior of superconductors. These equations describe the electromagnetic response of a superconductor to an applied magnetic field. They are given by:

$$
\nabla \times \mathbf{J} = -i\frac{e}{\hbar c} \mathbf{A}
$$

$$
\nabla \times \mathbf{B} = \mu_0 \mathbf{J}
$$

where $\mathbf{J}$ is the current density, $\mathbf{A}$ is the vector potential, $\mathbf{B}$ is the magnetic field, $e$ is the elementary charge, $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, $c$ is the speed of light, and $\mu_0$ is the vacuum permeability.

These equations describe the perfect diamagnetism observed in superconductors. When a superconductor is cooled below its transition temperature, it expels all magnetic fields from its interior, a phenomenon known as the Meissner effect. This is a direct consequence of London's equations, which show that the current density $\mathbf{J}$ is proportional to the vector potential $\mathbf{A}$. Since the vector potential is related to the magnetic field $\mathbf{B}$ through the equation $\mathbf{B} = \nabla \times \mathbf{A}$, the expulsion of magnetic fields from the superconductor can be understood as a consequence of the perfect diamagnetism described by London's equations.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of London's equations for the behavior of superconductors in the presence of magnetic fields.

#### 2.1b Meissner effect

The Meissner effect, named after the physicist Walther Meissner, is a fundamental property of superconductors that describes the expulsion of magnetic fields from the interior of a superconductor. This effect is a direct consequence of London's equations and is a key factor in the practical applications of superconductivity.

When a superconductor is cooled below its transition temperature, it enters a state of perfect diamagnetism. This means that it exhibits zero electrical resistance and expels all magnetic fields from its interior. This expulsion of magnetic fields is known as the Meissner effect.

The Meissner effect can be understood as a consequence of London's equations. The equations describe the electromagnetic response of a superconductor to an applied magnetic field. When a magnetic field is applied to a superconductor, it induces a current density $\mathbf{J}$ in the superconductor. According to London's equations, this current density is proportional to the vector potential $\mathbf{A}$. Since the vector potential is related to the magnetic field $\mathbf{B}$ through the equation $\mathbf{B} = \nabla \times \mathbf{A}$, the expulsion of magnetic fields from the superconductor can be understood as a consequence of the perfect diamagnetism described by London's equations.

The Meissner effect has significant implications for the practical applications of superconductivity. For instance, it allows for the creation of superconducting magnets that can generate extremely strong magnetic fields. These magnets have a wide range of applications, from medical imaging to particle accelerators.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of the Meissner effect for the behavior of superconductors in the presence of magnetic fields.

#### 2.1c Magnetic field expulsion in type I superconductors

In the previous section, we discussed the Meissner effect, a fundamental property of superconductors that describes the expulsion of magnetic fields from the interior of a superconductor. This effect is a direct consequence of London's equations and is a key factor in the practical applications of superconductivity.

In this section, we will delve deeper into the behavior of superconductors in the presence of magnetic fields, focusing on type I superconductors. Type I superconductors are a class of superconductors that exhibit a single critical temperature below which they transition from a normal conducting state to a superconducting state.

When a type I superconductor is cooled below its transition temperature, it enters a state of perfect diamagnetism, as we have discussed. This means that it expels all magnetic fields from its interior, a phenomenon known as the Meissner effect. However, the behavior of type I superconductors is more complex than this simple expulsion of magnetic fields.

In the presence of a magnetic field, a type I superconductor can exist in two different states: the Meissner state and the mixed state. In the Meissner state, the superconductor expels all magnetic fields from its interior, as we have discussed. In the mixed state, however, the superconductor allows some magnetic fields to penetrate its interior, but these fields are confined to small regions known as vortices.

The behavior of type I superconductors in the presence of magnetic fields can be understood in terms of the Ginzburg-Landau theory, a phenomenological theory that describes the behavior of superconductors near their transition temperature. According to this theory, the free energy of a superconductor in the presence of a magnetic field can be written as:

$$
F = \frac{1}{2}\alpha(T-T_c)\int d^3x |\psi|^2 + \frac{1}{4}\beta(T-T_c)^2\int d^3x |\psi|^4 + \frac{1}{2\mu_0}\int d^3x |\nabla\times\mathbf{B}|^2
$$

where $\psi$ is the wave function of the superconductor, $T$ is the temperature, $T_c$ is the critical temperature, $\alpha$ and $\beta$ are material-dependent constants, and $\mathbf{B}$ is the magnetic field.

The first two terms on the right-hand side of this equation describe the superconducting state, while the third term describes the magnetic field. The first term favors the superconducting state, while the second term favors the normal conducting state. The third term favors the expulsion of magnetic fields, leading to the Meissner effect.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of the Ginzburg-Landau theory for the behavior of superconductors in the presence of magnetic fields.




#### 2.2a Ginzburg-Landau theory

The Ginzburg-Landau theory is a phenomenological theory that describes the behavior of superconductors near the critical temperature. It was developed by Lev Landau and Vitaly Ginzburg in 1950 and is based on the concept of order parameter, a complex-valued function that describes the state of a superconductor.

The Ginzburg-Landau theory is based on the following assumptions:

1. The order parameter $A(\vec{r})$ is a complex-valued function that describes the state of a superconductor. It is zero in the normal state and non-zero in the superconducting state.
2. The order parameter is smooth and varies continuously across the transition temperature.
3. The free energy of a superconductor can be expressed as a functional of the order parameter.

The Ginzburg-Landau theory provides a mathematical framework for understanding the behavior of superconductors near the critical temperature. It is particularly useful for understanding the phenomenon of superconductivity in thin films and nanostructures, where the effects of surface and interface states can be significant.

The Ginzburg-Landau theory is based on the following equations:

$$
\alpha(\vec{r}) = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{\kappa^2} \right) \left| A(\vec{r}) \right|^2 + \frac{1}{4\kappa^2} \left| A(\vec{r}) \right|^4
$$

$$
\beta(\vec{r}) = \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{1}{2} - \frac{1}{\kappa^2} \right) \left| A(\vec{r}) \right|^2 - \frac{1}{4\kappa^2} \left| A(\vec{r}) \right|^4
$$

where $\alpha(\vec{r})$ and $\beta(\vec{r})$ are the real and imaginary parts of the order parameter, respectively, and $\kappa$ is a material-dependent parameter that characterizes the sharpness of the transition from the normal to the superconducting state.

The Ginzburg-Landau theory has been instrumental in the development of modern superconductivity. It has been used to understand the behavior of superconductors near the critical temperature, to predict the properties of superconductors, and to design new materials with improved superconducting properties.

#### 2.2b Macroscopic wave functions

The macroscopic wave functions are a key concept in the Ginzburg-Landau theory. They describe the collective behavior of the superconducting electrons in a macroscopic scale, rather than the microscopic behavior of individual electrons.

The macroscopic wave functions are defined as follows:

$$
\Psi(\vec{r}) = \sqrt{\frac{m}{2\hbar}} \left( A(\vec{r}) e^{i\theta(\vec{r})} \right) \phi(\vec{r})
$$

where $m$ is the electron mass, $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, $A(\vec{r})$ is the order parameter, $\theta(\vec{r})$ is the phase of the order parameter, and $\phi(\vec{r})$ is the wave function of the electrons.

The macroscopic wave functions are used to calculate the current density in the superconductor:

$$
\vec{J}(\vec{r}) = \frac{\hbar}{2mi} \left( \Psi^* \nabla \Psi - \Psi \nabla \Psi^* \right)
$$

where $\Psi^*$ is the complex conjugate of the wave function, and $\nabla$ is the gradient operator.

The macroscopic wave functions are also used to calculate the energy of the superconductor:

$$
E = \int \left[ \frac{\hbar^2}{2m} \left| (\nabla - \frac{ie}{\hbar c} \vec{A}) \Psi \right|^2 + \alpha(\vec{r}) \left| A(\vec{r}) \right|^2 + \beta(\vec{r}) \left| A(\vec{r}) \right|^4 \right] d\vec{r}
$$

where $e$ is the elementary charge, $c$ is the speed of light, and $\vec{A}$ is the vector potential.

The macroscopic wave functions provide a powerful tool for understanding the behavior of superconductors. They allow us to calculate the current density and the energy of the superconductor, and to understand the collective behavior of the superconducting electrons.

#### 2.2c Macroscopic wave functions

The macroscopic wave functions are a key concept in the Ginzburg-Landau theory. They describe the collective behavior of the superconducting electrons in a macroscopic scale, rather than the microscopic behavior of individual electrons.

#### 2.2b Coherence length and penetration depth

The coherence length and penetration depth are two fundamental concepts in the study of superconductivity. They provide a quantitative measure of the spatial extent of superconductivity in a material.

The coherence length, denoted as $l_c$, is a measure of the spatial extent over which the phase of the superconducting wave function is coherent. It is a key parameter in the Ginzburg-Landau theory and is defined as:

$$
l_c = \frac{\hbar}{\sqrt{2m(E-E_c)}}
$$

where $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, $m$ is the effective mass of the electron, $E$ is the energy of the electron, and $E_c$ is the critical energy. The coherence length is inversely proportional to the square root of the energy difference between the electron's energy and the critical energy. This means that at higher energies, the coherence length decreases, indicating a more incoherent superconducting state.

The penetration depth, denoted as $\lambda$, is a measure of the spatial extent over which the superconducting wave function penetrates into a normal metal. It is defined as:

$$
\lambda = \frac{1}{\sqrt{\mu_0 \mu_r \lambda_L}}
$$

where $\mu_0$ is the vacuum permeability, $\mu_r$ is the relative permeability, and $\lambda_L$ is the London penetration depth. The penetration depth is inversely proportional to the square root of the product of the vacuum permeability, the relative permeability, and the London penetration depth. This means that at higher permeabilities and London penetration depths, the penetration depth decreases, indicating a more localized superconducting state.

#### 2.2c Macroscopic wave function

The macroscopic wave function, also known as the Ginzburg-Landau wave function, is a fundamental concept in the study of superconductivity. It provides a mathematical description of the superconducting state, and its properties are crucial for understanding the behavior of superconductors.

The macroscopic wave function, denoted as $\Psi$, is a complex-valued function that describes the collective behavior of the superconducting electrons in a material. It is defined as:

$$
\Psi = \sqrt{\frac{m}{2\hbar}}\left(\psi_1 + \mathrm{i}\psi_2\right)e^{\mathrm{i}\theta}
$$

where $m$ is the effective mass of the electron, $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, $\psi_1$ and $\psi_2$ are the real-valued components of the wave function, and $\theta$ is the phase of the wave function. The macroscopic wave function is a function of space and time, and it describes the collective behavior of the superconducting electrons in a material.

The macroscopic wave function is a key component of the Ginzburg-Landau theory, which is a phenomenological theory that describes the behavior of superconductors near the critical temperature. The Ginzburg-Landau theory is based on the concept of the order parameter, which is a complex-valued function that describes the degree of superconductivity in a material. #### 2.3a Josephson effect

The Josephson effect is a quantum mechanical phenomenon that describes the behavior of superconducting materials. It is named after physicist Brian D. Josephson, who first predicted it in 1962. The effect is characterized by the ability of a superconductor to maintain a constant phase difference across an insulating barrier, even in the absence of any external electromagnetic field. This phenomenon is fundamental to the operation of many superconducting devices, including SQUIDs (Superconducting Quantum Interference Devices) and Josephson voltage standards.

The Josephson effect can be understood in terms of the Josephson equations, which describe the behavior of a superconductor across an insulating barrier. These equations are given by:

$$
\begin{align*}
I &= I_c \sin(\varphi) \\
\frac{d\varphi}{dt} &= \frac{\hbar}{2e} \left( \frac{I}{I_c} - \frac{V}{V_J} \right)
\end{align*}
$$

where $I$ is the current, $I_c$ is the critical current of the superconductor, $\varphi$ is the phase difference across the insulating barrier, $V$ is the voltage, $V_J$ is the Josephson voltage, $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, and $e$ is the elementary charge.

The first equation, known as the DC Josephson equation, describes the direct current crossing the insulator in the absence of any external electromagnetic field. This current is proportional to the sine of the phase difference, and can take values between $-I_c$ and $I_c$.

The second equation, known as the AC Josephson equation, describes the behavior of the superconductor when a fixed voltage $V_{DC}$ is applied across the junction. In this case, the phase will vary linearly with time, and the current will be a sinusoidal AC with amplitude $I_c$ and frequency $K_J V_{DC}$. This means that a Josephson junction can act as a perfect voltage-to-frequency converter.

The third effect, known as the inverse AC Josephson effect, describes the behavior of the superconductor when it is subjected to microwave radiation of a single frequency $\omega$. In this case, the phase takes the form $\varphi (t) = \varphi_0 + n \omega t + a \sin(\omega t)$, and the voltage and current across the junction are given by:

$$
\begin{align*}
V(t) &= \frac{\hbar}{2e} \omega (n + a \cos(\omega t)), \\
I(t) &= I_c \sum_{m = -\infty}^\infty J_m (a) \sin (\varphi_0 + (n + m) \omega t),
\end{align*}
$$

where $J_m (a)$ are the Bessel functions of the first kind. The DC components of the voltage and current are given by:

$$
\begin{align*}
V_\text{DC} &= n \frac{\hbar}{2e} \omega, \\
I_\text{DC} &= I_c J_{-n} (a) \sin \varphi_0.
\end{align*}
$$

This means that a Josephson junction can act like a perfect frequency-to-voltage converter, which is the theoretical basis for the Josephson voltage standard.

In the next section, we will discuss the applications of the Josephson effect in superconducting devices.

#### 2.3b Critical current

The critical current, denoted as $I_c$, is a fundamental concept in the study of superconductivity. It is the maximum current that can flow through a superconductor without causing it to lose its superconducting state. The critical current is a key parameter in the Josephson equations, and it plays a crucial role in the operation of many superconducting devices.

The critical current is determined by the properties of the superconductor, including its material composition, temperature, and magnetic field. For a superconductor in the Meissner state, the critical current is given by the equation:

$$
I_c = \frac{\Phi_0}{2\pi \xi}
$$

where $\Phi_0 = \frac{h}{2e}$ is the flux quantum, $\xi$ is the coherence length of the superconductor, and $h$ is the Planck's constant. The coherence length is a measure of the spatial extent over which the superconducting wave function is coherent. It is inversely proportional to the critical current, meaning that a superconductor with a shorter coherence length will have a higher critical current.

The critical current is also affected by the temperature and the magnetic field. As the temperature increases, the critical current decreases. This is due to the fact that the superconducting state becomes less stable at higher temperatures, and the superconductor may transition to a normal conducting state. Similarly, a magnetic field can reduce the critical current by disrupting the superconducting wave function.

The critical current is a key parameter in the design and operation of superconducting devices. For example, in a superconducting quantum interference device (SQUID), the critical current determines the sensitivity of the device to magnetic fields. In a Josephson voltage standard, the critical current determines the frequency response of the device. Understanding and controlling the critical current is therefore essential for the development of advanced superconducting technologies.

#### 2.3c Shapiro effect

The Shapiro effect is a phenomenon in superconductivity that describes the interaction of two superconducting waves. It is named after physicist Norman Shapiro, who first predicted it in 1956. The Shapiro effect is a key concept in the study of superconducting transmission lines, and it plays a crucial role in the operation of many superconducting devices.

The Shapiro effect occurs when two superconducting waves interact with each other. This interaction can cause the phase of one wave to change, leading to a change in the current flowing through the superconductor. The Shapiro effect is described by the Shapiro equation, which is given by:

$$
\Delta I = \frac{I_c}{2\pi} \sin(\Delta \varphi)
$$

where $\Delta I$ is the change in current, $I_c$ is the critical current, and $\Delta \varphi$ is the phase difference between the two waves. The Shapiro equation shows that the change in current is proportional to the sine of the phase difference, and it can take values between $-I_c$ and $I_c$.

The Shapiro effect has many applications in superconductivity. For example, it is used in the design of superconducting quantum interference devices (SQUIDs), which are highly sensitive to magnetic fields. The Shapiro effect allows SQUIDs to detect very small changes in magnetic fields, making them useful in a variety of applications, including medical imaging and geophysical exploration.

The Shapiro effect also plays a crucial role in the operation of superconducting transmission lines. These lines are used to transmit large amounts of electrical power with minimal losses. The Shapiro effect allows the power to be transmitted over long distances without significant loss, making superconducting transmission lines a promising technology for the future of energy transmission.

In the next section, we will discuss the applications of the Shapiro effect in superconducting devices.

#### 2.3d Josephson effect in superconductors

The Josephson effect is a quantum mechanical phenomenon that describes the behavior of superconductors. It is named after physicist Brian D. Josephson, who first predicted it in 1962. The Josephson effect is a key concept in the study of superconductivity, and it plays a crucial role in the operation of many superconducting devices.

The Josephson effect occurs when a superconductor is placed across an insulating barrier. This barrier prevents the flow of electric current, but it allows the superconducting wave function to tunnel through. The Josephson effect is described by the Josephson equations, which are given by:

$$
\begin{align*}
I &= I_c \sin(\varphi) \\
\frac{d\varphi}{dt} &= \frac{\hbar}{2e} \left( \frac{I}{I_c} - \frac{V}{V_J} \right)
\end{align*}
$$

where $I$ is the current, $I_c$ is the critical current, $\varphi$ is the phase difference across the insulating barrier, $V$ is the voltage, $V_J$ is the Josephson voltage, $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, and $e$ is the elementary charge.

The first equation, known as the DC Josephson equation, describes the direct current crossing the insulator in the absence of any external electromagnetic field. This current is proportional to the sine of the phase difference, and it can take values between $-I_c$ and $I_c$.

The second equation, known as the AC Josephson equation, describes the behavior of the superconductor when a fixed voltage $V_{DC}$ is applied across the junction. In this case, the phase will vary linearly with time, and the current will be a sinusoidal AC with amplitude $I_c$ and frequency $K_J V_{DC}$. This means that a Josephson junction can act as a perfect voltage-to-frequency converter.

The Josephson effect has many applications in superconductivity. For example, it is used in the design of superconducting quantum interference devices (SQUIDs), which are highly sensitive to magnetic fields. The Josephson effect allows SQUIDs to detect very small changes in magnetic fields, making them useful in a variety of applications, including medical imaging and geophysical exploration.

The Josephson effect also plays a crucial role in the operation of superconducting transmission lines. These lines are used to transmit large amounts of electrical power with minimal losses. The Josephson effect allows the power to be transmitted over long distances without significant loss, making superconducting transmission lines a promising technology for the future of energy transmission.

#### 2.3b AC Josephson effect

The AC Josephson effect is a direct consequence of the Josephson equations and is a fundamental property of superconductors. It describes the behavior of a superconductor when it is subjected to an alternating current (AC) voltage. This effect is particularly important in the operation of superconducting quantum interference devices (SQUIDs) and Josephson voltage standards.

When a superconductor is subjected to an AC voltage, the phase difference across the insulating barrier, $\varphi$, varies linearly with time. This results in an AC current with amplitude $I_c$ and frequency $K_J V_{DC}$. The AC Josephson effect can be described by the following equation:

$$
I = I_c \sin(\varphi)
$$

where $I$ is the current, $I_c$ is the critical current of the superconductor, and $\varphi$ is the phase difference across the insulating barrier.

The AC Josephson effect has several important implications. First, it allows a superconductor to act as a perfect voltage-to-frequency converter. This is because the frequency of the AC current is directly proportional to the voltage applied across the superconductor. This property is used in many applications, including Josephson voltage standards, which are used to measure voltage with high precision.

Second, the AC Josephson effect allows a superconductor to act as a perfect frequency-to-voltage converter. This is because the voltage across the superconductor is directly proportional to the frequency of the AC current. This property is used in many applications, including Josephson frequency standards, which are used to measure frequency with high precision.

Finally, the AC Josephson effect allows a superconductor to act as a perfect voltage-to-phase converter. This is because the phase difference across the insulating barrier is directly proportional to the voltage applied across the superconductor. This property is used in many applications, including SQUIDs, which are used to measure magnetic fields with high sensitivity.

In the next section, we will discuss the inverse AC Josephson effect, which describes the behavior of a superconductor when it is subjected to microwave radiation.

It describes the behavior of a superconductor when it is subjected to a fixed voltage $V_{DC}$ across the junction. In this case, the phase will vary linearly with time and the current will be a sinusoidal AC with amplitude $I_c$ and frequency $K_J V_{DC}$. This means that a Josephson junction can act as a perfect voltage-to-frequency converter.

The Josephson effect in superconductors can be described by the following equation:

$$
I = I_c \sin(\varphi)
$$

where $I$ is the current, $I_c$ is the critical current of the superconductor, and $\varphi$ is the phase difference across the insulating barrier.

The Josephson effect in superconductors has several important implications. First, it allows a superconductor to act as a perfect voltage-to-frequency converter. This is because the frequency of the AC current is directly proportional to the voltage applied across the superconductor. This property is used in many applications, including Josephson voltage standards, which are used to measure voltage with high precision.

Second, the Josephson effect in superconductors allows a superconductor to act as a perfect frequency-to-voltage converter. This is because the voltage across the superconductor is directly proportional to the frequency of the AC current. This property is used in many applications, including Josephson frequency standards, which are used to measure frequency with high precision.

Finally, the Josephson effect in superconductors allows a superconductor to act as a perfect voltage-to-phase converter. This is because the phase difference across the insulating barrier is directly proportional to the voltage applied across the superconductor. This property is used in many applications, including SQUIDs, which are used to measure magnetic fields with high sensitivity.

In the next section, we will discuss the inverse AC Josephson effect, which describes the behavior of a superconductor when it is subjected to microwave radiation.

#### 2.3c DC-SQUID

A DC-SQUID (Superconducting Quantum Interference Device) is a type of superconducting quantum interference device that operates in the DC regime. It is a highly sensitive magnetic field detector and has a wide range of applications, including in quantum computing and magnetic resonance imaging (MRI).

The operation of a DC-SQUID is based on the AC Josephson effect discussed in the previous section. When a magnetic field is applied to a DC-SQUID, it induces a phase difference across the insulating barrier, which results in an AC current with amplitude $I_c$ and frequency $K_J V_{DC}$. This AC current can be detected and measured, providing a sensitive means of detecting magnetic fields.

The DC-SQUID consists of a superconducting loop interrupted by two Josephson junctions. When a magnetic field is applied perpendicular to the loop, it induces a phase difference across the junctions, resulting in an AC current. The frequency of this AC current is directly proportional to the applied magnetic field, making the DC-SQUID a highly sensitive magnetic field detector.

The DC-SQUID has several important properties that make it useful in various applications. First, it has a high sensitivity to magnetic fields, allowing it to detect extremely small magnetic fields. This makes it particularly useful in quantum computing, where the manipulation of quantum states is highly sensitive to external disturbances.

Second, the DC-SQUID has a high bandwidth, allowing it to detect rapid changes in magnetic fields. This makes it useful in applications such as MRI, where rapid changes in magnetic fields are used to image the internal structure of the body.

Finally, the DC-SQUID has a high quantum coherence, meaning that it can maintain a quantum state for a long period of time. This makes it useful in quantum computing, where quantum states need to be manipulated and measured with high precision.

In conclusion, the DC-SQUID is a powerful tool in the field of applied superconductivity, with a wide range of applications in quantum computing and magnetic imaging. Its operation is based on the AC Josephson effect, making it a direct consequence of the Josephson equations.




#### 2.4a Josephson junctions in series

In the previous section, we discussed the DC-SQUID, a device that operates in the DC regime and is highly sensitive to magnetic fields. The operation of the DC-SQUID is based on the AC Josephson effect, which is a direct consequence of the Josephson equations. In this section, we will delve deeper into the Josephson equations and explore the behavior of Josephson junctions in series.

The Josephson equations describe the behavior of a superconducting circuit with a Josephson junction. The equations are given by:

$$
I = I_c \sin(\varphi)
$$

$$
\varphi = \frac{h}{2e} \int I dt
$$

where $I$ is the current through the junction, $I_c$ is the critical current of the junction, $\varphi$ is the phase difference across the junction, $h$ is Planck's constant, $e$ is the elementary charge, and $t$ is time.

When two Josephson junctions are connected in series, the total current through the circuit is given by:

$$
I = I_1 + I_2
$$

where $I_1$ and $I_2$ are the currents through the individual junctions. The phase difference across the junctions is also additive, and the total phase difference is given by:

$$
\varphi = \varphi_1 + \varphi_2
$$

This additivity of current and phase difference is a direct consequence of Kirchhoff's circuit laws, as discussed in the previous section.

The behavior of Josephson junctions in series can be understood in terms of the AC Josephson effect. When an external electromagnetic field is applied to the junctions, it induces a phase difference across the junctions, resulting in an AC current with amplitude $I_c$ and frequency $K_J V_{DC}$. This AC current can be detected and measured, providing a means of detecting external electromagnetic fields.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of Josephson junctions in series, including their use in superconducting quantum interference devices and their role in quantum computing.

#### 2.4b Josephson junctions in parallel

In the previous section, we discussed the behavior of Josephson junctions in series. Now, we will explore the behavior of Josephson junctions in parallel. 

When two Josephson junctions are connected in parallel, the voltage across the junctions is the same, and the total current through the circuit is given by:

$$
I = I_1 + I_2
$$

where $I_1$ and $I_2$ are the currents through the individual junctions. The phase difference across the junctions is also additive, and the total phase difference is given by:

$$
\varphi = \varphi_1 + \varphi_2
$$

This additivity of current and phase difference is a direct consequence of Kirchhoff's circuit laws, as discussed in the previous section.

The behavior of Josephson junctions in parallel can be understood in terms of the DC Josephson effect. When there is no external electromagnetic field, the DC Josephson effect results in a direct current crossing the insulator. This DC Josephson current is proportional to the sine of the phase difference across the junctions, and it can take values between $-I_c$ and $I_c$.

The DC Josephson effect can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
I = I_c \sin(\varphi)
$$

where $I$ is the current, $I_c$ is the critical current of the junction, and $\varphi$ is the phase difference across the junction.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of Josephson junctions in parallel, including their use in superconducting quantum interference devices and their role in quantum computing.

#### 2.4c Josephson junctions in parallel and in series

In the previous sections, we have discussed the behavior of Josephson junctions in series and in parallel. Now, we will explore the behavior of Josephson junctions when they are connected in both series and parallel.

When two Josephson junctions are connected in series and parallel, the total current through the circuit is given by:

$$
I = I_1 + I_2
$$

where $I_1$ and $I_2$ are the currents through the individual junctions. The phase difference across the junctions is also additive, and the total phase difference is given by:

$$
\varphi = \varphi_1 + \varphi_2
$$

This additivity of current and phase difference is a direct consequence of Kirchhoff's circuit laws, as discussed in the previous section.

The behavior of Josephson junctions in series and parallel can be understood in terms of the AC Josephson effect. When an external electromagnetic field is applied to the junctions, it induces a phase difference across the junctions, resulting in an AC current with amplitude $I_c$ and frequency $K_J V_{DC}$. This AC current can be detected and measured, providing a means of detecting external electromagnetic fields.

The AC Josephson effect can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
I = I_c \sin(\varphi)
$$

where $I$ is the current, $I_c$ is the critical current of the junction, and $\varphi$ is the phase difference across the junction.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of Josephson junctions in series and parallel, including their use in superconducting quantum interference devices and their role in quantum computing.




#### 2.4b Josephson junctions in parallel

In the previous section, we discussed the behavior of Josephson junctions in series. Now, we will explore the behavior of Josephson junctions in parallel.

When two Josephson junctions are connected in parallel, the total voltage across the junctions is given by:

$$
V = V_1 + V_2
$$

where $V_1$ and $V_2$ are the voltages across the individual junctions. The total current through the junctions is also additive, and the total current is given by:

$$
I = I_1 + I_2
$$

where $I_1$ and $I_2$ are the currents through the individual junctions. This additivity of voltage and current is a direct consequence of Kirchhoff's circuit laws, as discussed in the previous section.

The behavior of Josephson junctions in parallel can be understood in terms of the AC Josephson effect. When an external electromagnetic field is applied to the junctions, it induces a phase difference across the junctions, resulting in an AC current with amplitude $I_c$ and frequency $K_J V_{DC}$. This AC current can be detected and measured, providing a means of detecting external electromagnetic fields.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of Josephson junctions in parallel, including their use in superconducting quantum interference devices and their role in quantum computing.




#### 2.4c Shapiro steps

In the previous section, we discussed the behavior of Josephson junctions in parallel. Now, we will explore a phenomenon known as Shapiro steps, which is a direct consequence of the AC Josephson effect.

Shapiro steps are a series of discrete voltage steps that occur when a microwave signal is applied to a Josephson junction. These steps are named after the physicist Norman Shapiro, who first observed them in 1969.

The Shapiro steps occur due to the interaction between the microwave signal and the DC voltage applied to the junction. The microwave signal induces a phase difference across the junction, which results in an AC current with a frequency that is an integer multiple of the microwave frequency. This AC current can be detected and measured, providing a means of detecting the microwave signal.

The voltage at which the Shapiro steps occur can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
V = n \cdot \frac{h}{2e} \cdot f
$$

where $V$ is the voltage, $n$ is the integer number of the step, $h$ is Planck's constant, $e$ is the elementary charge, and $f$ is the frequency of the microwave signal.

The Shapiro steps are a crucial tool in the study of Josephson junctions and superconductivity. They provide a means of measuring the frequency of microwave signals with high precision, and they have been used in a variety of applications, including quantum computing and precision spectroscopy.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of Shapiro steps in more detail.




#### 2.5a Superconducting quantum interference devices

Superconducting quantum interference devices (SQUIDs) are a type of superconducting quantum device that exploit the quantum mechanical properties of superconductors. They are used in a variety of applications, including quantum computing, quantum sensing, and quantum metrology.

SQUIDs are essentially superconducting quantum devices that are used to measure extremely small magnetic fields. They are based on the principle of quantum interference, which is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics. In quantum interference, the wave function of a quantum system can be represented as a superposition of multiple states, each with a certain probability amplitude. When these states are measured, they can interfere constructively or destructively, leading to a change in the overall state of the system.

In the case of SQUIDs, the quantum system is a superconductor, and the states are the two possible states of the superconductor: the superconducting state and the normal state. When a magnetic field is applied to the superconductor, it can cause the superconductor to transition from the superconducting state to the normal state, leading to a change in the overall state of the system. This change can be measured, providing a means of detecting the magnetic field.

The operation of SQUIDs is based on the Josephson effect, which is a quantum mechanical effect that occurs in superconductors. The Josephson effect allows for the flow of supercurrent between two superconductors, even in the absence of an external electromagnetic field. This effect is used in SQUIDs to create a superconducting loop that can be used to measure extremely small magnetic fields.

SQUIDs have a wide range of applications in quantum computing. They can be used to measure the state of a qubit, which is a fundamental unit of quantum information. They can also be used to perform quantum operations, such as quantum gates, which are essential for performing quantum algorithms.

In the next section, we will explore the applications of SQUIDs in more detail, focusing on their use in quantum computing.

#### 2.5b Superconducting quantum computing

Superconducting quantum computing is a promising field that leverages the principles of quantum mechanics to perform computations. Unlike classical computers, which use bits to represent information as either 0 or 1, quantum computers use quantum bits or qubits. These qubits can exist in a superposition of states, allowing quantum computers to perform calculations much faster than classical computers.

Superconducting quantum computing is based on the principles of superconductivity, which is the phenomenon where certain materials exhibit zero electrical resistance and perfect diamagnetism when cooled below a critical temperature. This critical temperature is typically very low, often below 10 K, and is a major challenge in the field of superconducting quantum computing.

One of the primary challenges of superconducting quantum computing is the extremely low temperatures at which superconductors like Bose-Einstein Condensates exist. Other basic challenges in superconducting qubit design are shaping the potential well and choosing particle mass such that energy separation between two specific energy levels is unique, differing from all other interlevel energy separation in the system, since these two levels are used as logical states of the qubit.

Superconducting quantum computing must also mitigate quantum noise (disruptions of the system caused by its interaction with an environment) as well as leakage (information being lost to the surrounding environment). One way to reduce leakage is with parity measurements. Many current challenges faced by superconducting quantum computing lie in the field of microwave engineering. As superconducting quantum computing approaches larger scale devices, researchers face difficulties in qubit coherence, scalable calibration software, efficient determination of fidelity of quantum states across an entire chip, and qubit and gate fidelity. Moreover, superconducting quantum computing devices must be reliably reproducable at increasingly large scales such that they are compatible with these improvements.

The Journey of Superconducting Quantum Computing:

Although not the newest development, the focus began to shift onto superconducting qubits in the latter half of the 1990s when quantum tunneling across Josephson junctions became apparent which allowed for the realization that quantum computing could be achieved through these superconducting qubits.

At the end of the century in 1999, a paper was published by Yasunobu Nakamura, which exhibited the initial design of a superconducting qubit which is now known as the "charge qubit". This is the primary basis point on which later designs amended upon. These initial qubits had their limitations in terms of scalability and coherence, but they paved the way for the development of more advanced superconducting qubits.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the principles of superconducting qubits and their role in superconducting quantum computing.

#### 2.5c Superconducting quantum interference devices

Superconducting quantum interference devices (SQUIDs) are a type of superconducting quantum device that exploit the quantum mechanical properties of superconductors. They are used in a variety of applications, including quantum computing, quantum sensing, and quantum metrology.

SQUIDs are essentially superconducting quantum devices that are used to measure extremely small magnetic fields. In the case of SQUIDs, the quantum system is a superconductor, and the states are the two possible states of the superconductor: the superconducting state and the normal state. When a magnetic field is applied to the superconductor, it can cause the superconductor to transition from the superconducting state to the normal state, leading to a change in the overall state of the system. This change can be measured, providing a means of detecting the magnetic field.

The operation of SQUIDs is based on the Josephson effect, which is a quantum mechanical effect that occurs in superconductors. The Josephson effect allows for the flow of supercurrent between two superconductors, even in the absence of an external electromagnetic field. In the next section, we will explore the applications of SQUIDs in more detail, focusing on their use in quantum computing.

## Chapter: Superconducting quantum interference devices

### Introduction

Superconducting quantum interference devices, or SQUIDs, are a class of superconducting quantum devices that exploit the quantum mechanical properties of superconductors. They are used in a variety of applications, including quantum computing, quantum sensing, and quantum metrology. This chapter will provide a comprehensive guide to SQUIDs, covering their principles of operation, fabrication techniques, and applications in quantum technology.

SQUIDs are essentially superconducting quantum devices that are used to measure extremely small magnetic fields. They are based on the principle of quantum interference, which is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics. In quantum interference, the wave function of a quantum system can be represented as a superposition of multiple states, each with a certain probability amplitude. When these states are measured, they can interfere constructively or destructively, leading to a change in the overall state of the system.

In the case of SQUIDs, the quantum system is a superconductor, and the states are the two possible states of the superconductor: the superconducting state and the normal state. When a magnetic field is applied to the superconductor, it can cause the superconductor to transition from the superconducting state to the normal state, leading to a change in the overall state of the system. This change can be measured, providing a means of detecting the magnetic field.

The operation of SQUIDs is based on the Josephson effect, which is a quantum mechanical effect that occurs in superconductors. The Josephson effect allows for the flow of supercurrent between two superconductors, even in the absence of an external electromagnetic field. This effect is used in SQUIDs to create a superconducting loop that can be used to measure extremely small magnetic fields.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the principles of operation of SQUIDs, their fabrication techniques, and their applications in quantum technology. We will also discuss the challenges and future prospects of SQUIDs in the rapidly evolving field of quantum computing.




#### 2.5b DC-SQUID applications

DC-SQUIDs (Direct Current Superconducting Quantum Interference Devices) are a type of SQUID that operate in the direct current regime. They are used in a variety of applications, including quantum computing, quantum sensing, and quantum metrology.

DC-SQUIDs are essentially superconducting quantum devices that are used to measure extremely small magnetic fields. They are based on the principle of quantum interference, which is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics. In quantum interference, the wave function of a quantum system can be represented as a superposition of multiple states, each with a certain probability amplitude. When these states are measured, they can interfere constructively or destructively, leading to a change in the overall state of the system.

In the case of DC-SQUIDs, the quantum system is a superconductor, and the states are the two possible states of the superconductor: the superconducting state and the normal state. When a magnetic field is applied to the superconductor, it can cause the superconductor to transition from the superconducting state to the normal state, leading to a change in the overall state of the system. This change can be measured, providing a means of detecting the magnetic field.

The operation of DC-SQUIDs is based on the Josephson effect, which is a quantum mechanical effect that occurs in superconductors. The Josephson effect allows for the flow of supercurrent between two superconductors, even in the absence of an external electromagnetic field. This effect is used in DC-SQUIDs to create a superconducting loop that can be used to measure extremely small magnetic fields.

DC-SQUIDs have a wide range of applications in quantum computing. They can be used to measure the state of a qubit, which is a fundamental unit of quantum information. They can also be used to perform quantum operations, such as quantum gates, which are essential for performing quantum algorithms.

In addition to quantum computing, DC-SQUIDs are also used in quantum sensing and metrology. They can be used to measure extremely small magnetic fields, which is crucial in applications such as magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) and magnetic field mapping. They are also used in quantum metrology, which is a field that aims to improve the precision of quantum measurements.

In conclusion, DC-SQUIDs are a powerful tool in the field of quantum computing, sensing, and metrology. Their ability to measure extremely small magnetic fields and their compatibility with superconducting quantum devices make them an essential component in the development of quantum technologies. As research in this field continues to advance, we can expect to see even more exciting applications of DC-SQUIDs in the future.





#### 2.5c RF-SQUID applications

RF-SQUIDs (Radio Frequency Superconducting Quantum Interference Devices) are a type of SQUID that operate in the radio frequency regime. They are used in a variety of applications, including quantum computing, quantum sensing, and quantum metrology.

RF-SQUIDs are essentially superconducting quantum devices that are used to measure extremely small magnetic fields. They are based on the principle of quantum interference, similar to DC-SQUIDs. However, RF-SQUIDs operate in the radio frequency regime, which allows for higher sensitivity and faster response times compared to DC-SQUIDs.

RF-SQUIDs are used in a variety of applications, including:

1. Quantum computing: RF-SQUIDs are used to measure the state of a qubit, which is a fundamental unit of quantum information. They can also be used to perform quantum operations, such as quantum gates, which are essential for performing quantum algorithms.

2. Quantum sensing: RF-SQUIDs are used to detect extremely small magnetic fields, which is crucial in many quantum sensing applications. For example, they can be used to detect the magnetic fields produced by the spin of an electron, which is essential in quantum metrology.

3. Quantum metrology: RF-SQUIDs are used in quantum metrology to measure the state of a quantum system with high precision. This is crucial in many quantum information processing applications, such as quantum key distribution and quantum teleportation.

RF-SQUIDs are also used in a variety of other applications, including:

1. Magnetic resonance imaging (MRI): RF-SQUIDs are used in MRI machines to detect the magnetic fields produced by the spin of protons in the body. This allows for the creation of detailed images of the body's internal structures.

2. Superconducting quantum interference devices (SQUIDs): RF-SQUIDs are used in SQUIDs to measure extremely small magnetic fields. This is crucial in many applications, including the detection of extremely small magnetic fields produced by the spin of an electron.

3. Superconducting quantum computing: RF-SQUIDs are used in superconducting quantum computing to measure the state of a qubit and perform quantum operations. This is crucial in the development of quantum computers, which have the potential to solve complex problems that are currently intractable for classical computers.

In conclusion, RF-SQUIDs are a powerful tool in the field of quantum information processing. Their ability to measure extremely small magnetic fields with high precision makes them essential in a variety of applications, including quantum computing, quantum sensing, and quantum metrology. As technology continues to advance, it is likely that RF-SQUIDs will play an even more important role in the development of quantum technologies.

They are used in a wide range of applications, from high-speed data transmission to power transmission in superconducting networks. Understanding the principles behind transmission lines is essential for anyone working in the field of applied superconductivity.

# Title: Applied Superconductivity: A Comprehensive Guide

## Chapter 3: Waveguides




### Introduction

Superconductivity is a phenomenon that has been studied extensively since its discovery in 1911. It is a state of matter where certain materials exhibit zero electrical resistance and perfect diamagnetism when cooled below a critical temperature. This critical temperature, known as the transition temperature, is different for each material and is a key factor in determining the practical applications of superconductivity.

In this chapter, we will delve into the topic of generalized Josephson junctions, which are an essential component in many superconducting devices. Josephson junctions are formed when two superconducting materials are separated by a thin insulating layer. They exhibit a unique property known as the Josephson effect, where a supercurrent can flow between the two materials without any applied voltage.

We will begin by discussing the basics of Josephson junctions, including their structure and properties. We will then move on to explore the different types of Josephson junctions, such as the single junction, the double junction, and the multi-junction. We will also cover the effects of external magnetic fields and currents on Josephson junctions.

Next, we will delve into the topic of generalized Josephson junctions, which are junctions that exhibit a more complex behavior compared to traditional Josephson junctions. These junctions can exhibit multiple Josephson effects, such as the Shapiro effect and the Josephson-Plateau effect. We will also discuss the applications of generalized Josephson junctions in superconducting quantum computing and quantum information processing.

Finally, we will touch upon the future prospects of generalized Josephson junctions, including their potential use in high-speed computing and their role in advancing our understanding of quantum mechanics. We will also discuss the challenges and limitations of using generalized Josephson junctions and potential solutions to overcome them.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to generalized Josephson junctions, covering their theory, applications, and future prospects. It is our hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for researchers and students in the field of applied superconductivity.




### Subsection: 3.1a Josephson parametric amplifiers

Josephson parametric amplifiers are a type of Josephson circuit that utilizes the Josephson effect to amplify weak signals. They are widely used in various applications, including in quantum computing and quantum information processing.

#### The Josephson Parametric Amplifier

The Josephson parametric amplifier is a type of Josephson circuit that utilizes the Josephson effect to amplify weak signals. It consists of a Josephson junction, a resonant circuit, and a biasing circuit. The Josephson junction is biased at a voltage slightly above its critical voltage, and the resonant circuit is tuned to the Josephson frequency of the junction.

When a weak signal is applied to the resonant circuit, it modulates the Josephson frequency of the junction. This modulation results in a change in the Josephson current, which is then amplified by the resonant circuit. The amplified signal is then outputted from the circuit.

#### The Josephson Parametric Amplifier in Quantum Computing

In quantum computing, Josephson parametric amplifiers are used to amplify the signals from quantum gates. This is necessary because the signals from quantum gates are often very weak and need to be amplified to be detected. The Josephson parametric amplifier is particularly useful in this context because it can amplify the signals without introducing additional noise.

The Josephson parametric amplifier is also used in quantum information processing, where it is used to measure the state of quantum systems. By amplifying the signals from the quantum system, the Josephson parametric amplifier allows for more accurate measurements to be made.

### Subsection: 3.1b Josephson mixers

Josephson mixers are another type of Josephson circuit that is widely used in various applications. They are particularly useful in quantum computing and quantum information processing, where they are used to convert between different frequency signals.

#### The Josephson Mixer

The Josephson mixer is a type of Josephson circuit that utilizes the Josephson effect to convert between different frequency signals. It consists of two Josephson junctions, a resonant circuit, and a biasing circuit. The two Josephson junctions are biased at voltages slightly above their critical voltages, and the resonant circuit is tuned to the Josephson frequency of one of the junctions.

When a signal is applied to the resonant circuit, it modulates the Josephson frequency of the junction that is tuned to the same frequency. This modulation results in a change in the Josephson current, which is then mixed with the Josephson current from the other junction. The resulting signal is then outputted from the circuit.

#### The Josephson Mixer in Quantum Computing

In quantum computing, Josephson mixers are used to convert between different frequency signals. This is necessary because quantum gates often operate at different frequencies, and it is important to be able to convert between these frequencies. The Josephson mixer allows for this conversion to be done without introducing additional noise.

The Josephson mixer is also used in quantum information processing, where it is used to measure the state of quantum systems. By converting between different frequency signals, the Josephson mixer allows for more accurate measurements to be made.

#### The Josephson Mixer in Quantum Computing

In quantum computing, Josephson mixers are used to convert between different frequency signals. This is necessary because quantum gates often operate at different frequencies, and it is important to be able to convert between these frequencies. The Josephson mixer allows for this conversion to be done without introducing additional noise.

The Josephson mixer is also used in quantum information processing, where it is used to measure the state of quantum systems. By converting between different frequency signals, the Josephson mixer allows for more accurate measurements to be made.




### Subsection: 3.1c Josephson voltage standards

Josephson voltage standards are a crucial tool in the field of applied superconductivity. They are used to accurately measure and calibrate voltage levels, and are particularly useful in high-frequency applications.

#### The Josephson Voltage Standard

The Josephson voltage standard is a type of Josephson circuit that is used to accurately measure and calibrate voltage levels. It consists of a Josephson junction, a resonant circuit, and a biasing circuit. The Josephson junction is biased at a voltage slightly above its critical voltage, and the resonant circuit is tuned to the Josephson frequency of the junction.

When a voltage is applied to the resonant circuit, it modulates the Josephson frequency of the junction. This modulation results in a change in the Josephson current, which can be measured and used to determine the voltage level.

#### The Josephson Voltage Standard in Quantum Computing

In quantum computing, Josephson voltage standards are used to accurately measure and calibrate voltage levels. This is necessary because quantum gates often operate at high frequencies, and it is important to be able to accurately measure and control these voltage levels.

The Josephson voltage standard is also used in quantum information processing, where it is used to measure the state of quantum systems. By accurately measuring the voltage levels, the state of the system can be determined, allowing for more precise measurements and calculations.
